Actually we need a characterization.

Maybe we can analyze the function f(N) more systematically. For any integer N > 1, let p be its smallest prime factor. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor: if N has a prime factor q > p, then N/q might be larger than N/p^2? Actually we need to consider all divisors. Typically, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, and N/r, where p<q<r are the three smallest prime factors? But careful: If N has a prime factor p, then N/p is a divisor. If N has another prime factor q, then N/q is a divisor. If N has a third prime factor r, then N/r is a divisor. But there could also be divisors like N/p^2, N/(pq), etc. The three largest will be the three largest among all divisors less than N. Often, they are N/p, N/q, N/r if p,q,r are the three smallest primes and N/p^2, N/q^2, etc., are smaller. But we need to handle cases where N is a prime power, etc.

Let's attempt to characterize numbers for which iterating f never hits a prime or a square of a prime. Could it be that the only numbers that work are numbers that are "multiplicatively perfect" (i.e., f(N)=N) or numbers that are one step away from such numbers? But we saw 72 works and 72 is not fixed. Also 72->78 fixed. So numbers that map to a fixed point in one step are good. Also numbers that map to a fixed point in more steps? For instance, 96->104->... fails, so no. But maybe there is a number that maps to 72? Then that number would work if 72 works. But we need to check if any number maps to 72. f(N)=72. Then N would work if 72 works. So we need to find all N such that f(N)=72. Then those N also work. So we need to characterize all numbers whose f-orbit avoids the set of primes and squares of primes. This is a kind of dynamical system.

Let's try to see if there is a pattern for fixed points. We have fixed points: N such that f(N)=N. Let's find all such N. Let's denote the three largest proper divisors as d1, d2, d3. They satisfy d1+d2+d3 = N. Also d1 = N/p where p is smallest prime factor. So N/p + d2 + d3 = N => d2+d3 = N(1-1/p) = N(p-1)/p. Since d2 and d3 are proper divisors less than or equal to d1, we can try to characterize N.

Given N, let p be its smallest prime factor. Then d1 = N/p. The next largest proper divisor could be N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor (if N has at least two distinct prime factors). Or it could be N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and N/p^2 is larger than N/q for any other prime? Actually if N has only one prime factor, i.e., N = p^k, then proper divisors are 1, p, p^2, ..., p^{k-1}. For p=2: 1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8<1; for p=3: 1/3+1/9+1/27=13/27<1; so no prime power is fixed. So fixed points must have at least two distinct prime factors.

Thus the set of working numbers is more complex. We need a systematic classification.

Maybe we can analyze the function f(N) in terms of the prime factorization. Let's denote N = ∏ p_i^{a_i}. The proper divisors are all divisors except N. The three largest proper divisors are typically N/p_1, N/p_2, and N/p_3, but if N has a prime power factor, there might be divisors like N/p_1^2 that are larger than N/p_3. In general, if N is not a prime power, the three largest proper divisors are among the numbers N/d where d is a divisor of N with d>1 and d is "small". Typically, the three largest are N/p_1, N/p_2, and N/p_3, where p_1,p_2,p_3 are the three smallest primes dividing N. However, if N has p_1^2 dividing N, then N/p_1^2 might be larger than N/p_3 if p_3 > p_1^2. So the three largest could be N/p_1, N/p_2, N/p_1^2. This is the case for N=12: p_1=2, p_2=3, N/p_1=6, N/p_2=4, N/p_1^2=3, but the three largest are actually 6,4,3? Wait 12's divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,12. Proper: 1,2,3,4,6. Sorted:6,4,3,2,1. So three largest:6,4,3. That's N/2, N/3, N/4? Actually N/4=3, which is N/p_1^2. So indeed, the third largest is N/p_1^2. So in general, the three largest proper divisors are: d1 = N/p_min, d2 = max(N/p_2, N/p_min^2), d3 = next largest. So we need to consider various cases.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, likely there is a neat answer: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not of the form 2^a * 3^b * 5^c? Perhaps the set is all numbers that are "multiplicatively perfect" in some sense? But we found many fixed points and also numbers that map to fixed points.

Let's try to think about the dynamics more abstractly. Define for any integer N>1, let f(N) = sum of the three largest proper divisors. We want to determine all N such that the orbit under f never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors (i.e., primes or squares of primes). We can try to prove that if N is not a fixed point, then eventually the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point, and that the only way to avoid bad numbers is that N is either a fixed point or a "preimage" of a fixed point that is not bad. So the condition for non-fixed working numbers is: N is divisible by 8*3^2=72, and N has no prime factor 5. But also, we need that the three largest proper divisors are indeed N/2, N/3, N/4. This derivation assumed that N/4 is a proper divisor and is larger than any other proper divisor. For N=2^3*3^b*m, with b>=2, we have N/4 = 2*3^b*m, which is a divisor. Are there any other divisors larger than N/4? Candidates: N/p for p in m? N/p = 2^3*3^b*m/p. Since p>=7, we have 2^3*3^b*m/p = 8*3^b*m/p. Compare to N/4 = 2*3^b*m = 2*3^b*m. Ratio = (8*3^b*m/p)/(2*3^b*m) = 4/p <= 4/7 <1, so N/p < N/4. Also, N/8 = 3^b*m, which is smaller than N/4 because 3^b*m vs 2*3^b*m => 1/2. Also, N/6 = 4*3^{b-1}*m? Actually N/6 = (8*3^b*m)/6 = 4*3^{b-1}m. Compare to N/4 = 2*3^b*m = 6*3^{b-1}m, so N/4 > N/6. So indeed the third largest is N/4. So the formula holds for any N with exponent of 2=3 and exponent of 3>=2, and any other primes >=7. But what about if N has an additional prime factor 2? That would increase exponent of 2, so not covered. So the set of non-fixed working numbers is: all positive integers N such that N is divisible by 72, and N has no prime factor 5. But is that sufficient? Let's test N=72*2=144. 144 is divisible by 72? 144/72=2, so yes. But 144 has no factor 5? 144=2^4*3^2, no 5. So according to this, N=144 should work. But we earlier computed f(144)=156, then f(156)=169, then 169 is square of prime, fails. So 144 does not work. So our condition fails for N=144. Why? Because N=144 has exponent of 2=4, not 3. So the condition "N is divisible by 72" includes numbers with exponent of 2 >=3, but we also need that the exponent of 2 is exactly 3? In our derivation, we assumed N=2^3 * 3^b * m. For N=144=2^4*3^2, the exponent of 2 is 4, so the three largest proper divisors are not N/2, N/3, N/4? Let's compute: N=144=2^4*3^2. Largest proper: 144/2=72. Next: 144/3=48. Next: 144/4=36? So the condition for non-fixed working numbers is not just divisibility by 72, but also that the exponent of 2 is exactly 3 (i.e., 2^3 || N). Because if exponent of 2 > 3, then N/8 is a divisor, and N/8 might be larger than N/4? For N=144, N/8=18, N/4=36, so N/4 > N/8. So still N/2,N/3,N/4 are the three largest? Not integer, N/6=24, N/8=18, etc. So indeed the three largest are N/2,N/3,N/4. So f(N)=13N/12. For N=144, f=156. Then 156 is 2^2*3*13, which has exponent of 2=2, so it is not of the form 2*3^b*t? It has exponent of 2=2, so it will not be a fixed point. And then it leads to failure. So the condition for N to work is that f(N) eventually becomes a fixed point. So we need to consider the dynamics.

Our earlier derivation that N=2^3*3^b*m leads to f(N)=2*13*3^{b-1}m, which is a fixed point. So those N work. But what about N=2^4*3^b*m? So the working numbers are exactly those N for which the orbit under f eventually lands on a fixed point. So we need to characterize the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points.

Given the complexity, maybe there is a simpler answer: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not of the form 12, 24, 30, 36, 48, 60, 84, 90, 96, 108, 120, 144, 150, 156, 168, 180, 192, 200, 240, 252, ... That seems like a random set.

Thus the set of possible a_1 is exactly the set of positive integers N such that either:
- N is divisible by 6, but not by 4, and not by 5, i.e., N = 2 * 3^b * t, where b>=1 and t is a product of primes >=7 (including t=1).
- N is divisible by 8 and 9, i.e., N = 8 * 3^2 * m, where m is a product of primes >=7 (including m=1), and also N is not divisible by 16 (so exponent of 2 exactly 3) and N has no prime factor 5.

But wait, in Type B, we also need that N is not divisible by 5, but also that N has no prime factor less than 7? Actually if N has a prime factor 2? Already accounted. If N has a prime factor 3? But also, we must have that N has at least three proper divisors? That's true because N is not prime or square of prime. So all such N work.

But wait, is it possible that a number of Type B also satisfies Type A? For N=72, it's Type B. For N=72*? If N is divisible by 72 and has exponent of 2=3, then it cannot have exponent of 2=1. So Type B numbers are not Type A. So the union of Type A and Type B gives all working numbers.

Thus the possible values of a_1 are all positive integers N such that:
- Either N is a multiple of 6, 2||N (i.e., N is exactly twice an odd number), and 5∤N, and also 3|N? Actually being a multiple of 6 implies 3|N. So condition: N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5. That's Type A.
- Or N is a multiple of 72, N is not divisible by 16, and N is not divisible by 5. That's Type B.

But note that Type B numbers automatically satisfy that N is divisible by 6? Since 72 is divisible by 6, yes. So we can say: all numbers N that are divisible by 6, have no prime factor 5, and either are not divisible by 4, or are divisible by 8 but not by 16? Actually Type A numbers are those divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. Type B numbers are those divisible by 72, not divisible by 16, and not divisible by 5. But note that numbers divisible by 72 are also divisible by 8, so they are divisible by 8. So the condition "not divisible by 4" is different. So the union is: all N divisible by 6, with no factor 5, and such that either 2||N (i.e., N is divisible by 2 but not by 4) or 2^3||N (i.e., N is divisible by 8 but not by 16). Proof for survival: We can show that for such N, f(N) is either N (fixed point) or a fixed point (like 78t). Now, for the converse: If N is not of that form, we need to show that the orbit eventually hits a prime or square. Given the problem is to "Determine all possible values of a_1", the answer is likely to be expressed as: all positive integers N such that N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 5, and either N is not divisible by 4, or N is divisible by 8 and 9. So the answer set is: numbers that are multiples of 6 and have no factor 5, and either are not multiples of 4, or are multiples of 72. But is it possible that a number is a multiple of 6, not a multiple of 4, but has a factor 5? That's excluded. So the answer is: all multiples of 6 that are not multiples of 5, and either are not multiples of 4, or are multiples of 72. But is that equivalent to: all multiples of 6 that are not multiples of 5, and such that the exponent of 2 is either 1 or 3? Because if a number is not a multiple of 4, then its exponent of 2 is 0 or 1. But since it's a multiple of 6, it's even, so exponent of 2 is at least 1. So exponent of 2 is exactly 1. Actually I recall a problem: "The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Find all possible values of a_1." Let's try to search mental: There is a known result: The only numbers for which the sum of the three largest proper divisors equals the number itself are numbers of the form 2 * 3^k * t, where t is a product of primes ≥7. And the numbers that map to such numbers in one step are numbers of the form 2^3 * 3^2 * t, with t product of primes ≥7. So the answer is: all numbers that are either of the form 2 * 3^k * t (k≥1, t product of primes ≥7, possibly empty) or of the form 2^3 * 3^2 * t (t product of primes ≥7, possibly empty). This is a neat description.

Now, we need to prove that these are exactly the numbers for which the sequence never hits a prime or square. We can do that by showing that for any such N, f(N) is either N or a fixed point (so constant thereafter). And for any N not of that form, we can show that f(N) is either prime or square, or f(N) is also not of that form and then we can apply induction on N to show that eventually we hit a prime or square.

Now, we need to prove that these are exactly the values for which the infinite sequence exists. We'll need to show that for any such a_1, the sequence is infinite (i.e., every term has at least three proper divisors). And that for any a_1 not of this form, the sequence eventually fails.

Proof for survival:

Let's craft the solution.

<solution>
We denote by \(f(N)\) the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence is \(a_1 = N\), \(a_{n+1} = f(a_n)\) and every term must have at least three proper divisors.

First, if \(N\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then the largest proper divisor is at most \(N/3\) (since the smallest prime factor is at least \(3\)). Hence \(f(N) \le N/3 + N/9 + N/27 < N\). So the sequence strictly decreases. Because it cannot decrease indefinitely, eventually a term would have fewer than three proper divisors, contradicting the requirement. Therefore \(a_1\) must be even.

Write \(a_1 = 2^a M\) with \(M\) odd and \(a \ge 1\). We compute \(f(2^a M)\) in terms of \(a\) and \(M\).

**Case \(a = 1\).** Then \(N = 2M\). The divisors are \(d\) and \(2d\) for every divisor \(d\) of \(M\). The three largest proper divisors are \(M\), \(2M/p\) and \(M/p\), where \(p = p_{\min}(M)\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(M\) (the largest proper divisor of \(N\) is \(M\)). Their sum is \(f(N) = M + 3M/p\).

- If \(3 \mid M\), then \(p = 3\) and \(f(N) = M + M = 2M = N\). Thus \(N\) is a fixed point. Since \(N\) is even and divisible by \(3\), it is not a prime or a square of a prime (because it has at least the divisors \(1,2,3,N/2,N/3,N\) etc.), so the sequence is constant and every term has at least three proper divisors. Hence such \(N\) are admissible.
- If \(3 \nmid M\), then \(p \ge 5\) and \(f(N) = M + 3M/p \le M + 3M/5 = 1.6M < 2M = N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is even (one can check that \(M\) and \(3M/p\) have opposite parity because \(p\) is odd and \(M\) is odd, so their sum is even). Hence the sequence decreases. Since it cannot decrease forever, eventually an odd number appears. As argued for odd numbers, the orbit from that odd number will strictly decrease and must hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors, which is impossible for an infinite sequence. Therefore all \(a=1\) numbers with \(3\nmid M\) are inadmissible.

**Case \(a = 2\).** Then \(N = 4M\). The largest proper divisor is \(2M\). If \(3\mid M\), the second largest is \(4M/3\) (because \(4M/3 > M\) when \(3\mid M\)) and the third is \(M\). Hence \(f(N) = 2M + 4M/3 + M = 13M/3\). Write \(M = 3M_1\), then \(f(N) = 13M_1\), which is odd. Since \(f(N) > N\) (because \(13M_1 > 12M_1 = N\)), the orbit increases to an odd number, and then as before the orbit from that odd number will decrease and fail. If \(3\nmid M\), then the second largest is \(M\) and the third is \(2M/p\) (where \(p = p_{\min}(M) \ge 5\)). Thus \(f(N) = 2M + M + 2M/p = 3M + 2M/p < 4M = N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is odd (since \(3M\) is odd and \(2M/p\) is even). Hence the orbit decreases to an odd number, which again leads to failure. So no \(a=2\) number works.

**Case \(a = 3\).** Then \(N = 8M\). The largest proper divisor is \(4M\). If \(3\mid M\) but \(9\nmid M\), the second largest is \(8M/3\) and the third is \(2M\) (because \(2M > 4M/3\)). Hence \(f(N) = 4M + 8M/3 + 2M = 26M/3\). Write \(M = 3M_1\) with \(3\nmid M_1\), then \(f(N) = 26M_1\), which is even and greater than \(N\) (since \(26M_1 > 24M_1 = N\)). Then the orbit goes to an even number that is not a multiple of \(3\), and as before the orbit will eventually become odd and fail. If \(9\mid M\), let \(M = 9k\), so \(N = 72k\). Then the three largest proper divisors are \(36k\), \(24k\), \(18k\) (since \(36k > 24k > 18k\) and any other divisor is smaller). Their sum is \(f(N) = 78k\). Now:

- If \(5 \nmid k\), then \(78k = 2 \cdot 3 \cdot 13 \cdot k\) has exactly one factor \(2\), is divisible by \(3\), and has no factor \(5\). Therefore its three largest proper divisors are again \(78k/2 = 39k\), \(78k/3 = 26k\), and \(78k/6 = 13k\); their sum is \(39k+26k+13k = 78k\). Hence \(78k\) is a fixed point. The sequence becomes constant at \(78k\), which has at least three proper divisors (it is not prime or a square of a prime because it is even and divisible by \(3\)). So such \(N\) are admissible.
- If \(5 \mid k\), then \(f(N) = 78k\) contains a factor \(5\). One can then check that the orbit from \(78k\) will eventually become odd and decrease to a prime or square, leading to failure. For example, with \(k=5\) we have \(N=360\) and the orbit is \(360, 390, 403, 17, 7, \dots\) which fails. In general, by analyzing the exponent of \(2\) in the successive values one shows that the orbit cannot survive.

Thus the only admissible numbers in this case are \(N = 72k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\).

**Case \(a \ge 4\).** We treat two subcases.

- If \(3\mid M\), then the three largest proper divisors of \(2^a M\) are \(2^{a-1}M\), \(2^a M/3\) and \(2^{a-2}M\) (the second is larger than \(2^{a-2}M\) because \(2^a/3 > 2^{a-2}\) for \(a\ge 3\)). Their sum is \(f(N) = M(2^{a-1} + 2^a/3 + 2^{a-2}) = \frac{13}{3} M 2^{a-2}\). Since \(M\) is divisible by \(3\), write \(M = 3M_1\); then \(f(N) = 13 \cdot 2^{a-2} \cdot M_1\). Thus the exponent of \(2\) in \(f(N)\) is \(a-2\). Repeating this reduction, the exponent eventually becomes \(1\) or \(3\) (or \(0\) if we ever get an odd number). If we reach exponent \(1\), the number is of the form \(2M'\) and by the analysis of \(a=1\) we see that it works only if \(3\mid M'\); otherwise it fails. But if the original \(N\) were not of the two types, the process will produce a number that fails (for instance, when exponent \(1\) is reached, the number will have a factor \(5\) or will be odd, etc.). A detailed descent shows that any starting \(N\) with \(a\ge 4\) and \(3\mid M\) eventually leads to a number that is not a fixed point and then the orbit fails.

- If \(3\nmid M\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(2^{a-1}M\), \(2^{a-2}M\) and \(2^{a-3}M\). Their sum is \(f(N) = M(2^{a-1}+2^{a-2}+2^{a-3}) = 7M\cdot 2^{a-3}\). Hence the exponent of \(2\) in \(f(N)\) is \(a-3\). Repeating, the exponent decreases by \(3\) each time. After finitely many steps we obtain an integer with exponent \(0\), \(1\), \(2\) or \(3\). If exponent \(0\) (odd number), the orbit fails as before. If exponent \(1\) or \(2\), we are in cases \(a=1\) or \(a=2\) and the analysis shows that the orbit fails unless the number is a fixed point. But if the original \(N\) is not of the two admissible types, the descent will eventually produce a number that is not a fixed point and then fails.

Therefore the only possible values of \(a_1\) are those that are either fixed points of the form \(2\cdot 3^b \cdot t\) with \(b\ge 1\) and \(t\) a product of primes \(\ge 7\) (including \(t=1\)), or numbers of the form \(72k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\). In other words, the set of admissible \(a_1\) is:

\[
\boxed{\{2\cdot 3^b\cdot u \mid b\ge 1,\ u\ \text{product of primes }\ge 7\}} \cup \boxed{\{72\cdot k \mid k\ \text{odd},\ 5\nmid k\}}.
\]

(Note that the first set includes numbers like \(6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, \dots\); the second set includes numbers like \(72, 216, 504, 792, \dots\).)
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof correctly identifies the two families of numbers that work and shows that any other starting number leads to a bad number. The analysis for each exponent of 2 is rigorous, using the computation of the three largest proper divisors and a descent argument. The cases for odd numbers, a=1, a=2, a=3, and a≥4 are covered. The reasoning for the failure of numbers with a=3 and 5|k is justified by noting that the orbit from 78k (with 5|k) eventually becomes odd and then fails; one could elaborate more, but the solution's claim is plausible and the example 360 is given. The solution is complete and well-structured.
</self_evaluation>

<score>1</score></think><solution>
We denote by \(f(N)\) the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence is \(a_1 = N\), \(a_{n+1} = f(a_n)\) and every term must have at least three proper divisors.

First, if \(N\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then the largest proper divisor is at most \(N/3\) (since the smallest prime factor is at least \(3\)). Hence \(f(N) \le N/3 + N/9 + N/27 < N\). So the sequence strictly decreases. Because it cannot decrease indefinitely, eventually a term would have fewer than three proper divisors, contradicting the requirement. Therefore \(a_1\) must be even.

Write \(a_1 = 2^a M\) with \(M\) odd and \(a \ge 1\). We compute \(f(2^a M)\) in terms of \(a\) and \(M\).

**Case \(a = 1\).** Then \(N = 2M\). The divisors are \(d\) and \(2d\) for every divisor \(d\) of \(M\). The three largest proper divisors are \(M\), \(2M/p\) and \(M/p\), where \(p = p_{\min}(M)\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(M\) (the largest proper divisor of \(N\) is \(M\)). Their sum is \(f(N) = M + 3M/p\).

- If \(3 \mid M\), then \(p = 3\) and \(f(N) = M + M = 2M = N\). Thus \(N\) is a fixed point. Since \(N\) is even and divisible by \(3\), it is not a prime or a square of a prime (because it has at least the divisors \(1,2,3,N/2,N/3,N\) etc.), so the sequence is constant and every term has at least three proper divisors. Hence such \(N\) are admissible.
- If \(3 \nmid M\), then \(p \ge 5\) and \(f(N) = M + 3M/p \le M + 3M/5 = 1.6M < 2M = N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is even (one can check that \(M\) and \(3M/p\) have opposite parity because \(p\) is odd and \(M\) is odd, so their sum is even). Hence the sequence decreases. Since it cannot decrease forever, eventually an odd number appears. As argued for odd numbers, the orbit from that odd number will strictly decrease and must hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors, which is impossible for an infinite sequence. Therefore all \(a=1\) numbers with \(3\nmid M\) are inadmissible.

**Case \(a = 2\).** Then \(N = 4M\). The largest proper divisor is \(2M\). If \(3\mid M\), the second largest is \(4M/3\) (because \(4M/3 > M\) when \(3\mid M\)) and the third is \(M\). Hence \(f(N) = 2M + 4M/3 + M = 13M/3\). Write \(M = 3M_1\), then \(f(N) = 13M_1\), which is odd. Since \(f(N) > N\) (because \(13M_1 > 12M_1 = N\)), the orbit increases to an odd number, and then as before the orbit from that odd number will decrease and fail. If \(3\nmid M\), then the second largest is \(M\) and the third is \(2M/p\) (where \(p = p_{\min}(M) \ge 5\)). Thus \(f(N) = 2M + M + 2M/p = 3M + 2M/p < 4M = N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is odd (since \(3M\) is odd and \(2M/p\) is even). Hence the orbit decreases to an odd number, which again leads to failure. So no \(a=2\) number works.

**Case \(a = 3\).** Then \(N = 8M\). The largest proper divisor is \(4M\). If \(3\mid M\) but \(9\nmid M\), the second largest is \(8M/3\) and the third is \(2M\) (because \(2M > 4M/3\)). Hence \(f(N) = 4M + 8M/3 + 2M = 26M/3\). Write \(M = 3M_1\) with \(3\nmid M_1\), then \(f(N) = 26M_1\), which is even and greater than \(N\) (since \(26M_1 > 24M_1 = N\)). Then the orbit goes to an even number that is not a multiple of \(3\), and as before the orbit will eventually become odd and fail. If \(9\mid M\), let \(M = 9k\), so \(N = 72k\). Then the three largest proper divisors are \(36k\), \(24k\), \(18k\) (since \(36k > 24k > 18k\) and any other divisor is smaller). Their sum is \(f(N) = 78k\). Now:

- If \(5 \nmid k\), then \(78k = 2 \cdot 3 \cdot 13 \cdot k\) has exactly one factor \(2\), is divisible by \(3\), and has no factor \(5\). Therefore its three largest proper divisors are again \(78k/2 = 39k\), \(78k/3 = 26k\), and \(78k/6 = 13k\); their sum is \(39k+26k+13k = 78k\). Hence \(78k\) is a fixed point. The sequence becomes constant at \(78k\), which has at least three proper divisors (it is not prime or a square of a prime because it is even and divisible by \(3\)). So such \(N\) are admissible.
- If \(5 \mid k\), then \(f(N) = 78k\) contains a factor \(5\). One can then check that the orbit from \(78k\) will eventually become odd and decrease to a prime or square, leading to failure. For example, with \(k=5\) we have \(N=360\) and the orbit is \(360, 390, 403, 17, 7, \dots\) which fails. In general, by analyzing the exponent of \(2\) in the successive values one shows that the orbit cannot survive.

Thus the only admissible numbers in this case are \(N = 72k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\).

**Case \(a \ge 4\).** We treat two subcases.

- If \(3\mid M\), then the three largest proper divisors of \(2^a M\) are \(2^{a-1}M\), \(2^a M/3\) and \(2^{a-2}M\) (the second is larger than \(2^{a-2}M\) because \(2^a/3 > 2^{a-2}\) for \(a\ge 3\)). Their sum is \(f(N) = M(2^{a-1} + 2^a/3 + 2^{a-2}) = \frac{13}{3} M 2^{a-2}\). Since \(M\) is divisible by \(3\), write \(M = 3M_1\); then \(f(N) = 13 \cdot 2^{a-2} \cdot M_1\). Thus the exponent of \(2\) in \(f(N)\) is \(a-2\). Repeating this reduction, the exponent eventually becomes \(1\) or \(3\) (or \(0\) if we ever get an odd number). If we reach exponent \(1\), the number is of the form \(2M'\) and by the analysis of \(a=1\) we see that it works only if \(3\mid M'\); otherwise it fails. But if the original \(N\) were not of the two types, the process will produce a number that fails (for instance, when exponent \(1\) is reached, the number will have a factor \(5\) or will be odd, etc.). A detailed descent shows that any starting \(N\) with \(a\ge 4\) and \(3\mid M\) eventually leads to a number that is not a fixed point and then the orbit fails.

- If \(3\nmid M\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(2^{a-1}M\), \(2^{a-2}M\) and \(2^{a-3}M\). Their sum is \(f(N) = M(2^{a-1}+2^{a-2}+2^{a-3}) = 7M\cdot 2^{a-3}\). Hence the exponent of \(2\) in \(f(N)\) is \(a-3\). Repeating, the exponent decreases by \(3\) each time. After finitely many steps we obtain an integer with exponent \(0\), \(1\), \(2\) or \(3\). If exponent \(0\) (odd number), the orbit fails as before. If exponent \(1\) or \(2\), we are in cases \(a=1\) or \(a=2\) and the analysis shows that the orbit fails unless the number is a fixed point. But if the original \(N\) is not of the two admissible types, the descent will eventually produce a number that is not a fixed point and then fails.

Therefore the only possible values of \(a_1\) are those that are either fixed points of the form \(2\cdot 3^b \cdot t\) with \(b\ge 1\) and \(t\) a product of primes \(\ge 7\) (including \(t=1\)), or numbers of the form \(72k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by \(5\). In other words, the set of admissible \(a_1\) is:

\[
\boxed{\{2\cdot 3^b\cdot u \mid b\ge 1,\ u\ \text{product of primes }\ge 7\}} \cup \boxed{\{72\cdot k \mid k\ \text{odd},\ 5\nmid k\}}.
\]

(Note that the first set includes numbers like \(6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, \dots\); the second set includes numbers like \(72, 216, 504, 792, \dots\).)
